Title: Tesla, Rivian, and Lucid shares fall as Trump kicks off his anti-EV agenda
URL: https://qz.com/tesla-rivian-lucid-stock-trump-evs-gm-ford-stellantis-1851743803
Time Published: 2025-01-21T17:50:00Z
Full Content:
The Detroit automakersâ€™ shares are rising Tuesday while their fully-electric rivals saw their stocks fall as President Donald Trump began tearing into his predecessorâ€™s pro-EV moves. â€œWe will end the Green New Deal, and we will revoke the electric-vehicle mandate, saving our auto industry and keeping my sacred pledge to our great American auto workers,â€ Trump said in his inaugural address. â€œYouâ€™ll be able to buy the car of your choice.â€ Later on Monday, he issued an executive order to eliminate the â€œEV mandate, terminate state emissions rules that limit the sale of gasoline-powered cars, and consider eliminating any subsidies for EVs. There is no nationwide U.S. electric-vehicle mandate, but some states, led by California, have implemented their own rules to phase out fossil fuel-powered vehicles, while the Environmental Protection Agency has issued a rule limiting tailpipe emissions. Trump also directed agencies to stop handing out funds tied to Former President Joe Bidenâ€™s Inflation Reduction Act and Infrastructure Investment and Jobs Act, which provided funding for EVs and other clean energy infrastructure. More than $82.5 billion has been allocated to 164 EV-related projects, creating 63,659 jobs, through the IRA, according to E2's investment tracker. The president is also expected to scrap the IRAâ€™s $7,500 tax credit for EV buyers, which has helped keep costs down and boost sales for automakers. Electric truck maker Rivianâ€™s (RIVN-4.55%) stock fell by more than 6%, while luxury EV maker Lucidâ€™s (LCID-6.29%) shares dropped by more than 4%. Tesla (TSLA-1.75%), despite Elon Muskâ€™s close relationship with Trump and a price target raise from Piper Sandler, fell almost 3%. â€œNot sure how the math works for elimination of the EV credit to be bullish,â€ for Tesla, the Future Fund Managing Partner Gary Black wrote Monday. Black noted that Germany and Franceâ€™s moves to end their EV tax credits contributed to Teslaâ€™s sales volumes falling 41% and 34%, respectively, on an annual basis. The stock may also be down because of Muskâ€™s alignment with Trump, as well as reactions to a gesture he made while speaking at Trumpâ€™s inauguration parade on Monday. Tesla lost $15 billion in brand value in 2024, according to a new report, partially due to Muskâ€™s political rhetoric and brash behavior. The Detroit automakers will also be affected by Trumpâ€™s EV plans, but have their gas-powered and hybrid portfolios â€” which still account for most of their sales â€” to fall back on. Ford Motor Co. (F-3.60%) and Stellantis (STLA-0.57%) shares are up by almost 2% and more than 2%, respectively. General Motors (GM-2.02%) shares gained almost 5% in trading Tuesday, partially boosted by an upgrade from a â€œholdâ€ rating to â€œbuyâ€ from Deutsche Bank (DB-0.71%). Analysts led by Edison Yu say that GMâ€™s recent moves to close down its Cruise self-driving unit and restructure its China business, along with its recent buybacks, give them confidence in the company. â€œWhile there are concerns about the cycle and potential policies of the new Trump administration, our view is that these risks are already very well-known, and thereâ€™s room for positive surprises,â€ the analysts wrote on Tuesday. One such surprise could be Trump declining to follow through on his plans to slap Canadian and Mexican imports with tariffs, which would affect most automakers. Title: As Trump Takes the Wheel, Big Corporations Extricate From DEI, Climate Programs

After years of applying the progressive environmental, social, and governance (ESG) ideological framework to their corporations, executives appear to now be realizing that these programs could be driving their companies into a legal and financial wall. The past year has seen a growing list of Fortune 500 companies announcing that they are dropping race- and gender-based programs for their employees and pulling out of global net zero climate clubs. Companies that have announced they are canceling or dialing back their diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI) programs include Meta, Walmart, Ford, McDonaldâ€™s, Harley-Davidson, John Deere, Tractor Supply Company, Loweâ€™s, Molson Coors, Nissan, Toyota, and Stanley Black & Decker. In addition, within weeks of the 2024 election, six of the largest U.S. banksâ€”Goldman Sachs, Citigroup, Wells Fargo, JPMorgan Chase, Bank of America, and Morgan Stanleyâ€”dropped out of the U.N.-sponsored Net-Zero Banking Alliance. On Jan. 13, the Net Zero Asset Managers initiative (NZAMi) announced that it would suspend its activities after investor giant BlackRock announced its withdrawal from the club on Jan. 9. These departures followed in the wake of half of the members of the Net-Zero Insurance Alliance quitting that organization in 2023. This has led many to conclude that the ESG movement is rapidly coming to an end. â€œESG and DEI are both on death watch,â€ Daniel Cameron, former Kentucky attorney general and current CEO of the nonprofit 1792 Exchange, told The Epoch Times. â€œI think itâ€™s because companies are realizing that a lot of the country just want our corporate community to focus on creating and developing great products and providing great service, rather than pushing or preaching a partisan agenda,â€ he said. Republican victories in the November 2024 elections will likely spark more defections from the progressive corporate movement. â€œThe election of Donald Trump put backers of ESG on notice, and I believe the recent departure by several major banks and asset managers from the U.N.â€™s anti-fossil fuel cartel confirms that the broader ESG movement is on life-support,â€ Rep. Riley Moore (R-W.Va.) told The Epoch Times. ... In his former role as state treasurer of West Virginia, Moore was among the first to divest state funds from BlackRock over what he charged was the firmâ€™s support for ESG policies. The Nasdaq Stock Market on Jan. 16 filed a request with the Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC), seeking to withdraw its 2021 diversity rule after an appeals court recently struck it down. The Nasdaq rule had required companies listed on the exchange to have at least two â€œdiverseâ€ board members, including â€œat least one director who self-identifies as a femaleâ€ and one who â€œself-identifies as Black or African American, Hispanic or Latinx, Asian, Native American or Alaska Native, Native Hawaiian or Pacific Islander, two or more races or ethnicities, or as LGBTQ+.â€ Is ESG Good for Companies? The ESG ideology originated at the United Nations in 2004 as a way to get private companies on board with progressive climate and social goals known as the U.N.â€™s â€œSustainable Development Goals.â€ ... The worldâ€™s savings and investments are heavily concentrated in the hands of just a few fund managers, the largest of which, called the â€œBig Three,â€ are BlackRock, State Street, and Vanguard, which collectively manage more than $20 trillion in assets largely through their index funds. By comparison, the United Statesâ€™ GDP is approximately $29 trillion. ... Added to this are the United Statesâ€™ largest municipal retirement funds, including CalPERS, CalSTRS, and New York City and state pension funds, which have also pressured companies to embrace ESG ideology. These fund managers joined U.N.-sponsored net zero alliances, such as NZAMi and the Net-Zero Banking Allianceâ€”which pledged to reduce the use of fossil fuelsâ€”as well as other activist organizations such as Climate Action 100+. ... The Biden administration also threw its weight behind the ESG movement. In 2021, President Joe Biden signed an executive order to â€œadvance diversity, equity, inclusion, and accessibility in all parts of the Federal workforce.â€ In 2022, the U.S. Labor Department altered its rules to allow asset managers of private Employee Retirement Income Security Act (ERISA) pensions to consider â€œclimate change and other environmental, social, and governance factorsâ€ when investing pension money. Previously, under the first Trump administration, ERISA fund managers could only consider pecuniary factors that would maximize returns to pensioners. In addition, the SEC in March 2024 implemented what became known as the â€œgreen accountingâ€ rule, which required all listed companies to audit and document their â€œclimate-related risks,â€ their carbon dioxide emissions, and their strategies for reducing them. ... â€˜The Beginning of the Endâ€™.. President-elect Donald Trump has pledged to end DEI throughout the federal government and in federally funded colleges, and in anticipation, the FBI reportedly closed its Office of Diversity and Inclusion in December 2024. The incoming administration is also likely to reverse the SECâ€™s green accounting rule and reinstate its previous policies that ban non-pecuniary investing of ERISA pension funds. But according to ESG critics, the ideology still has life in it. ... â€œItâ€™s the beginning of the end,â€ Will Hild, executive director of Consumersâ€™ Research, told The Epoch Times. â€œThe momentum has shifted and I donâ€™t see it coming back, but thereâ€™s still a lot of work to do. â€œEven with BlackRock leaving Net Zero Asset Managers, and [NZAMi] shutting down operations, you still have a number of these firms, including BlackRock, who have not repudiated their firmâ€™s individual commitment to pushing net zero.â€ ... â€œWe already saw the customer and employee backlash happen with companies like Tractor Supply, John Deere, Harley-Davidson, etc. Now attention is being placed on legal and regulatory risks, especially with the American Airlines ruling that its 401(k) plans violate ERISA fiduciary duties.â€ .... On Jan. 13, a U.S. district court ruled that American Airlines had mishandled its employeesâ€™ retirement funds by allowing fund managers to pursue ESG investments. At the height of the ESG movement, many executives acted in lockstep, seemingly believing that â€œsafety in numbersâ€ shielded them from legal action, analysts said. But with every company that steps away from ESG ideology, those that remain committed are more exposed to employee and investor lawsuits and enforcement actions from federal and state officials, ... every company that abandons its ESG or DEI policies â€œmakes it harder for the remaining companies to toe the line.â€ In December 2023, Skrmetti brought a consumer-protection suit against BlackRock, alleging that the firm had misled Tennessee consumers regarding the extent to which ESG factors affected its investment strategies. ... The legal risks include violation of civil rights laws, antitrust laws, and consumer protection laws, experts said. Following the Supreme Court ruling in October 2022 that race-based admissions at Harvard and the University of North Carolina violated U.S. civil rights laws, 13 state attorneys general penned a letter to the United Statesâ€™ largest companies, stating, â€œthe Supreme Courtâ€™s recent decision should place every employer and contractor on notice of the illegality of racial quotas and race-based preferences in employment and contracting practices.â€ The letter warned that â€œif your company previously resorted to racial preferences or naked quotas to offset its bigotry, that discriminatory path is now definitively closed.â€ Corporate attorneys are beginning to push companies to acknowledge and disclose these risks to investors. In its September 2023 10-K filing with the SEC, for example, The Walt Disney Company listed among its risk factors the fact that â€œconsumersâ€™ perceptions of [Disneyâ€™s] position on matters of public interest, including ... efforts to achieve certain ... environmental and social goals, often differ widely and present risks to [Disneyâ€™s] reputation and brands.â€ ... This disclosure followed a significant decline in Disneyâ€™s sales and share price following the companyâ€™s campaign against a Florida parentsâ€™ rights law that barred teachers in grades Kâ€“3 from discussing sexual topics with students, as well as revelations that company producers were routinely putting controversial sexual themes into childrenâ€™s content. In November 2023, shareholders sued Target and its board of directors, alleging that they â€œbetrayed both Targetâ€™s core customer base of working families and its investors by making false and misleading statements about Targetâ€™s Environmental, Social and Governance (ESG) and Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion (DEI) mandates that led to its disastrous 2023 children-and-family themed LGBT-Pride campaign.â€ And in 2023, a white Starbucks employee was awarded $25 million when the company was found to have fired her because of her race. Companies put themselves at risk with race-based training and other policies, as these practices open them up to employee lawsuits for creating a hostile work environment, Hild said. â€œWhere were their compliance attorneys on this stuff?â€ he said. â€œI think it raises the question of whether a lot of these companies need to take a look at their compliance departments and have some firings on that front.â€ Anticipating that the Trump administration will take a tougher approach to enforcing civil rights and antitrust laws regarding ESG, many companies are now racing to get their policies in line with these laws. â€œA lot of companies got way out over their skis on this, but the law hasnâ€™t changed,â€ Skrmetti said. â€œYouâ€™re going to see a lot of litigation going forward, and thatâ€™s why I think youâ€™re seeing a lot of companies move very, very quickly to try to get on the right side of the law before the Trump administration begins enforcing these laws that have been on the books for a very long time. â€œWe are a country of individual Americans, and when you put people in buckets based on something as trivial as the color of their skin and treat some people differently than others, itâ€™s evil,â€ he said. This has led many to conclude that the ESG movement is rapidly coming to an end. â€œESG and DEI are both on death watch,â€ Daniel Cameron, former Kentucky attorney general and current CEO of the nonprofit 1792 Exchange, told The Epoch Times. â€œI think itâ€™s because companies are realizing that a lot of the country just want our corporate community to focus on creating and developing great products and providing great service, rather than pushing or preaching a partisan agenda,â€ he said. The ESG ideology originated at the United Nations in 2004 as a way to get private companies on board with progressive climate and social goals known as the U.N.â€™s â€œSustainable Development Goals.â€ ... The worldâ€™s savings and investments are heavily concentrated in the hands of just a few fund managers, the largest of which, called the â€œBig Three,â€ are BlackRock, State Street, and Vanguard, which collectively manage more than $20 trillion in assets largely through their index funds. By comparison, the United Statesâ€™ GDP is approximately $29 trillion. ... The legal risks include violation of civil rights laws, antitrust laws, and consumer protection laws, experts said.
